Simulations

Interpreting genomic offsets via extensive simulation

Motivation

Initially, genomic offset statistics did not have an explicit link with any theoretical model. It was not until 2023 that Gain et al. (2023) provided a theoretical model for it and proposed a unified theoretical justification of the existing metrics. They considered a locally optimal meta-population with a latent adaptive phenotype under Gaussian stabilizing selection whose optimal is a function of a set of causal environmental variables. They established that it is possible to estimate a proxy of the fitness under the novel environment and overpass the measurement of the latent phenotypic trait by fitting a proper genotype-environmental model with adaptive alleles and causal environmental variables.

For these theoretical results to apply, genomic offsets should be calculated, in principle, with the set of QTLs that control the hidden adaptive phenotype, as well as the set of environmental variables that control the optimal value of the trait. Moreover, the empirical dataset should include individuals (or populations) within their adaptive optimum across an environmental gradient that is appropriate to make the desired predictions.

These conditions are hardly ever fulfilled. Therefore, we propose a pragmatic approach where, rather than simply assuming the naive data model (and praying for the best), researchers carefully consider a set of plausible scenarios, estimate genomic offsets from simulated datasets, and interpret the empirical genomic offsets in the light of their simulations.

In our opinion, simulations should play a major role in the interpretation and justification of genomic offsets. However, designing and performing such simulations can be an obstacle. So as not to reinvent the wheel, here we provide a template that can be easily adapted to different cases, built on top of state-of-the-art simulation software.

Gain et al. (2023) model

As an illustrative example (and to later exemplify the usage of the package), we will simulate a dataset coming from the naive model proposed by Gain et al. (2023).

We advise the usage of SLiM, a state-of-the-art forward simulator, in conjunction with tree-sequence recording and msprime (a backward coalescence simulator) to facilitate the analysis and minimize the computational cost (Haller and Messer 2023; Baumdicker et al. 2021).

In addition to being flexible to accommodate many types of models and efficient, they provide extensive documentation we invite the reader to look at more an in-depth introduction. In that regard, it would be particularly relevant to the pyslim documentation. For further information regarding individual-based spatial simulations, please refer to Chevy et al. (2024).

Generating standing genetic diversity

All forward simulation software suffers from the issue that, the first generation of individuals is not related to each other. To address this, a neutral burnin is typically included as part of the simulations. Here, we propose another popular approach, based on on performing a coalescence simulation first to simulate the genealogies of the first generation. This approach proves also handy, as it provides a very elegant way of simulating realistic patterns of genetic variation in the QTLs we’ll use later in the forward simulation. If you want to simulate a hard-selective sweep (rather than a soft one), adding the mutations could be done manually in SLiM.

Simulating the entire first generation can become computationally expensive. An alternative approach (that scales better with the population size, especially if sampling a limited number of individuals from the final generation) is recapitation.

import msprime, pyslim, tskit, tstrait
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
rng = np.random.default_rng(1111)

First, we have to consider how large the initial population size should be. This decision depends enterily on your model. Here, we consider a population that has evolved neutrally for a long time, and that has already reached the carrying capacity of their habitat. Therefore, the initial population size should roughly match the one of the population at equilibirum. Let’s calculate that by assuming the density-carrying-capacity is \(K=10\) and we simulated landscape is a rectangular of size \(10\times 10\).

width, height = 10, 10 # Arbitrary distance units
K = 10 # Density carrying capacity
Ne0 = width * height * K
Ne0
1000

Now, we can simulate the ancestries of the initial population using msprime. We’ll simulate a single chromosome of length of 100Mb (and 100 cM).

seed = rng.integers(1, 2^32-1, 1)
ots = msprime.sim_ancestry(
    samples=Ne0, 
    population_size=Ne0,
    random_seed=seed,
    sequence_length=1e8,
    recombination_rate=1e-8
)
# Annotate tree-sequence with SLiM metadata
ots = pyslim.annotate(ots, model_type="nonWF", tick=1, stage="late")
ots

Now, we’ll superimpose over the edges of the tree sequences the mutations we’ll use later in the forward-simulation step to control our adaptive phenotype. However, we do not want to simulate all neutral mutations, as those can be simulated at the end, making our simulation more efficient. Let’s assume our phenotypic trait is controlled by \(L=500\) QTLs. To simulate them, we can use the following iterative approach1:

  1. Choose a very small mutation rate, \(\mu = 1e^{-15}\)
  2. Overlay mutations at the specified rate \(\mu\).
  3. If we have less than \(L\) varying sites, increase \(\mu\) by a factor of 2 and go to (2) again. Otherwise, continue to (4).
  4. Sample without replacement \(L\) varying sites and assign them appropriate effect sizes.
L = 500 # Number of desired QTLs
# Add mutations
mut_model = msprime.SLiMMutationModel(type=2) # Non-neutral mutations, m2
mu = 1e-15
while ots.num_sites  < L :
    seed = rng.integers(1, 2^32-1, 1)
    ots = msprime.sim_mutations(
        ots, rate=mu,
        model=mut_model, keep=True,
        random_seed=seed
    )
    mu = mu * 2
# Discard excess
num_exceeded = ots.num_sites - L
site_ids = [site.id for site in ots.sites()]
discard_ids = rng.choice(site_ids, num_exceeded, replace=False)
ots = ots.delete_sites(discard_ids)
ots

Adaptive phenotypic trait

To make the simulation more interesting, let’s simulate an adaptive phenotypic trait that is a bit more involved than the one considered by Gain and collaborators.

First, let’s say the phenotypic trait (that, until know, has been purely neutral) is controlled by the \(L\) QTLs. The vector of quantitative traits \(\mathbf z\) from an additive model is:
\[ \mathbf z = \mathbf Y\alpha+\epsilon, \] where \(\mathbf Y\) is the matrix that describes the number of causal alleles in each individual (with values equal to 0, 1 or 2), \(\alpha\) is the vector of effect sizes and \(\epsilon\) is the vector of environmental noise. Moreover, let’s consider that effect sizes are drawn from a Normal distribution:

\[ \beta \sim N(0, \sigma_z) \]

and environmental noise is distributed as

\[ \epsilon\sim N\left(0,V_G\cdot\frac{1-h^2}{h^2} \right), \]

where \(V_G=Var(Y\beta)\) and \(h^2\) is the narrow-sense heritability.

sigma_z = 0.1
h2 = 0.9

We’ll use the library tstrait (which is part of the tskit ecosystem, as well as msprime) to simulate the effect sizes, as it allows to easily explore different effect sizes distribution.

import tstrait
trait_model = tstrait.trait_model(distribution="normal", mean=0, var=sigma_z)
trait_df = tstrait.sim_trait(
    ots, num_causal=L, 
    model=trait_model,
    random_seed=seed
)
trait_df.head()
position site_id effect_size causal_allele allele_freq trait_id
0 210705 0 0.261639 115 0.0020 0
1 464156 1 0.560826 434 0.0025 0
2 705713 2 -0.124500 435 0.0015 0
3 1019951 3 0.321431 437 0.0005 0
4 1205417 4 0.373873 226 0.0005 0

We can further explore the simulated effect sizes:

plt.hist(trait_df["effect_size"], bins=40, color="firebrick")
plt.ylabel("Frequency")
plt.xlabel(r"$\beta$")
plt.title("Distribution of effect sizes")
plt.show()

And the distribution of the adaptive phenotypes of the initial population:

genetic_df = tstrait.genetic_value(ots, trait_df)
phenotype_df = tstrait.sim_env(genetic_df, h2=h2, random_seed=seed)
plt.hist(phenotype_df["phenotype"], bins=40, color="firebrick")
plt.ylabel("Frequency")
plt.xlabel(r"$z$")
plt.title("Distribution of phenotypic trait values")
plt.show()

Now, we have to annotate the mutations in our tree-sequence in a way SLiM can understand it later. Because of some particularities (mutation stacking, SLiM mutations that appear in more than one mutation in the tree sequence), the following code might not be straighforward. We refer to the pyslim documentation for more details.

# Get the simulated effect sizes
effect_sizes = trait_df["effect_size"]
# Mutable copy of the tree-sequence object
tables = ots.tables
tables.mutations.clear()
for m in ots.mutations():
    site_id = m.site
    md_list = m.metadata["mutation_list"]
    slim_ids = m.derived_state.split(",")
    assert len(slim_ids) == len(md_list)
    # For simplicity, we'll ignore recurrent mutations
    # in the same QTL (and consider only the first one)
    md_list[0]["selection_coeff"] = effect_sizes[site_id]
    # We'll consider the rest of the mutations, if any, neutral
    for md in md_list[1:]:
        md ["mutation_type"] = 1
    # Append the edited mutations
    _ = tables.mutations.append(
        m.replace(metadata={"mutation_list": md_list})
    )
assert tables.mutations.num_rows == ots.num_mutations

Happily, we are almost ready to be ready for loading the simulated tree-sequence in SLiM:

# Finish editing the metadata: 
ts_metadata = tables.metadata
ts_metadata["SLiM"]["spatial_dimensionality"] = "xy" # 2D landscape
tables.metadata = ts_metadata
ots = tables.tree_sequence()
ots.dump("init.trees")

Forward simulation

Now, we have to code the SLiM simulation (in a separate file, and in a special scripting language called Eidos). For resources on learning these, please refer to the extensive SLiM manual.

Spatial SLiM simulations can be intimidating at first, but hopefully, we can explain what’s going on in the following model.

  1. First, we start the simulation from the tree-sequence file generated using msprime.
  2. We simulate a heterogeneous landscape and place those individuals there.
  3. At each tick (an arbitrary time unit that should be close to the generation time, as we have set the average lifetime to be 1), each individual will:
    • Choose a mate close to each other with a probability drawn from a Gaussian
    • Have \(n\) children, drawn from a Poisson, that will be placed around their carrying parent according to another Gaussian.
    • Each individual will survive to the next tick according to their calculated viability.

The viability of each individual is calculated as the product of the effect of the density-dependent selection and the adaptive phenotypic trait under stabilizing selection.

  • The density-dependent selection is modeled (yet again) with a Gaussian kernel. The factor we compute for each individual can be interpreted as proportional to the time spent by all individuals near each individual.
  • The impact of the adaptive phenotypic trait is modeled (again!) so the viability of each individual is proportional to the fitness as computed with the equation

\[ w(z|x) \propto \exp\left(-\frac{(z-z_{\text{opt}}(x))^2}{2\sigma_z}\right) \]

where \(z\) is the phenotype, \(x\) is the environment, \(z_{\text{opt}}(x)\) is the optimum phenotype under environment \(x\) and \(\sigma_z\) is the width of the curve.

The resulting model could look something like this:

cat ../src/gain_model.slim
// This script is based on https://github.com/kr-colab/spatial_sims_standard
initialize() {
    initializeSLiMModelType("nonWF");
    initializeSLiMOptions(dimensionality="xy");
    initializeTreeSeq();
    defineConstant("INITFILE", "init.trees");
    defineConstant("OUTFILE", "out.trees");
    
    // Constant parameters
    defineConstant("LIFETIME", 1); // average life span
    defineConstant("SZ", 0.1); // sigma_Z, dispersal fitness Gaussian
    defineConstant("H2", 0.8); // h2, heritability
    defineConstant("SD", 0.3); // sigma_D, dispersal distance
    defineConstant("SX", 0.3); // sigma_X, interaction distance for measuring
    defineConstant("SM", 0.3); // sigma_M, mate choice distance
    defineConstant("K", 10); // carrying capacity per unit area
    defineConstant("WIDTH", 10.0); // width of the simulated area
    defineConstant("HEIGHT", 10.0); // height of the simulated area
    defineConstant("RUNTIME", 5000); // Number of ticks
    defineConstant("L", 1e8); // genome length
    defineConstant("R", 1e-8); // recombination rate    
    // Derived parameters
    defineConstant("FECUN", 1 / LIFETIME);
    defineConstant("RHO", FECUN / ((1 + FECUN) * K));
    
    // basic genetics
    initializeMutationRate(0.0);
    initializeMutationType("m1", 0.5, "f", 0.0); // neutral
    initializeMutationType("m2", 0.5, "f", 0.0); // QTLs
    m2.convertToSubstitution = F;
    initializeGenomicElementType("g1", m1, 1.0);
    initializeGenomicElement(g1, 0, L-1);
    initializeRecombinationRate(R);
    
    // spatial interaction for local density measurement
    initializeInteractionType(1, "xy", reciprocal=T, maxDistance=3 * SX);
    i1.setInteractionFunction("n", 1, SX);
    
    // spatial interaction for mate choice
    initializeInteractionType(2, "xy", reciprocal=T, maxDistance=3 * SM);
    i2.setInteractionFunction("n", 1, SM);

}

// We want to handle the impact of QTLs in fitness ourselves
mutationEffect(m2) { return 1.0; }

// Initialize the simulation
1 late() { initialize(); }

// preparation for the reproduction() callback
2: first() { i2.evaluate(p0); }

reproduction() {
    mate = i2.drawByStrength(individual, 1);
    if (mate.size())
        subpop.addCrossed(individual, mate, count=rpois(1, FECUN));
}

2: early() {
    // Disperse offspring
    offspring = p0.subsetIndividuals(maxAge=0);
    // Simulate phenotypes and record them
    offspring.tagF = simulatePhenotypes(offspring, m2);
    
    p0.deviatePositions(offspring, "reprising", INF, "n", SD);
    // Measure local density and use it for density regulation
    i1.evaluate(p0);
    inds = p0.individuals;
    competition = i1.localPopulationDensity(inds);
    inds.fitnessScaling = 1 / (1 + RHO * competition);
    phenotype = inds.tagF;
    env = p0.spatialMapValue(ENV,inds.spatialPosition);
    w = relativeFitness(inds.tagF, env);
    inds.fitnessScaling = inds.fitnessScaling * w;
    if ((sim.cycle-2) % 500 == 0)
        catn(sim.cycle-2+"\t"+mean((phenotype-optimumUnderEnv(env))^2));
}

late() {
    // Error handling
    if (p0.individualCount == 0) {
        catn("Population went extinct! Ending the simulation.");
        sim.simulationFinished();
    }
}

RUNTIME late() {
    catn("End of simulation (run time reached)");
    saveResults();
}

// Initialize the simulation
function (void) initialize(void) {
    sim.readFromPopulationFile(INITFILE);
    inds = p0.individuals;
    p0.setSpatialBounds(c(0, 0, WIDTH, HEIGHT));
    // Record positions & phenotypes
    positions = p0.pointUniform(p0.individualCount);
    inds.setSpatialPosition(positions);
    // Simulate heterogenous landscape
    mapValues = matrix(runif(25, -4, 4), ncol=5);
    map1 = p0.defineSpatialMap("map1", "xy", mapValues, interpolate=T);
    defineConstant("ENV", map1);
    // Simulate heterogenous landscape *after*
    mapValues2 =mapValues * runif(length(mapValues), -1, 1);
    map2 = p0.defineSpatialMap("map2", "xy", mapValues2, interpolate=T);
    defineConstant("ENV_ALTERED", map2);
    
    // Simulate phenotypes and record them
    inds.tagF = simulatePhenotypes(inds, m2);
    // Print header for logging purposes
    catn("Tick\tMeanSquaredDistance");
}

function (void) saveResults(void) {
    inds = p0.individuals;
    params = Dictionary(
        "x", inds.x,
        "y", inds.y,
        "PHENOTYPE", inds.tagF,
        "ENV", p0.spatialMapValue(ENV,inds.spatialPosition),
        "ENV_ALTERED", p0.spatialMapValue(ENV_ALTERED,inds.spatialPosition)
        );
    params.setValue(
        "FITNESS", relativeFitness(
        params.getValue("PHENOTYPE"),
        params.getValue("ENV")
        )
        );
    params.setValue(
        "FITNESS_ALTERED", relativeFitness(
        params.getValue("PHENOTYPE"),
        params.getValue("ENV_ALTERED")
        )
        );
    
    sim.treeSeqOutput(OUTFILE, metadata=params);
    sim.simulationFinished();

}

function (numeric)optimumUnderEnv(numeric env) {
    // For simplicity, identity function!
    return env;
}

function (numeric)relativeFitness(numeric pheno, numeric env) {
    return dnorm(pheno, optimumUnderEnv(env), SZ);
}

function (numeric)simulatePhenotypes(object<Individual> individuals, object<MutationType> mutation) {
    geneticValue = individuals.sumOfMutationsOfType(mutation);
    sigma = sqrt(var(geneticValue) * (1-H2)/H2);
    return geneticValue + rnorm(length(geneticValue), sd=sigma);
}

Let’s finally run the simulation:

slim -s 1000 ../src/gain_model.slim
// Initial random seed:
1000

// RunInitializeCallbacks():
initializeSLiMModelType(modelType = 'nonWF');
initializeSLiMOptions(dimensionality = 'xy');
initializeTreeSeq();
initializeMutationRate(0);
initializeMutationType(1, 0.5, "f", 0);
initializeMutationType(2, 0.5, "f", 0);
initializeGenomicElementType(1, m1, 1);
initializeGenomicElement(g1, 0, 99999999);
initializeRecombinationRate(1e-08);
initializeInteractionType(1, "xy", reciprocal=T, maxDistance=0.9);
initializeInteractionType(2, "xy", reciprocal=T, maxDistance=0.9);

// Starting run at tick <start>:
1

Datasets

Causal dataset

First, we’ll generate the ideal dataset, with causal environmental variables and the QTLs only. We’ll need four elements: 1. Current environmental matrix, \(\mathbf X\) 2. Altered environmental matrix, \(\mathbf X^*\) 3. Genotype matrix \(\mathbf Y\) 4. Altered fitness proxy vector \(w^*\)

X = np.array(df["ENV"]).reshape(df.shape[0], 1)
Xstar = np.array(df["ENV_ALTERED"]).reshape(df.shape[0], 1)
def genotype_matrix(ts):
    # The genotype_matrix produces one column per haplotype,
    # although we can easily fix this
    genos = ts.genotype_matrix()
    Y = np.zeros((ts.num_individuals, ts.num_sites), dtype = np.int32)
    for ind in ts.individuals():
        Y[ind.id, :] = np.sum(genos[:, ind.nodes], axis=1)
    return Y
Y = genotype_matrix(ts)
Y.shape
(2808, 219)
wstar = np.array(df["FITNESS_ALTERED"]).reshape(df.shape[0])

We have now generated our “causal” dataset. We’ll save it for later use.

np.savez(
    "../genomic_offsets/causal.npz", 
    X = X, Xstar = Xstar,
    Y = Y, wstar=wstar
)

Empirical dataset

To simulate an empirical dataset, we need to augment the causal dataset with (1) neutral loci and (2) unrelated environmental variables. Luckily, both are easy to do. Firs, we generate the new environmental matrices:

randomX = np.array(rng.uniform(-4, 4, (X.shape[0], 3)))
randomXstar = randomX + rng.uniform(-1, 1, randomX.shape)
# Augment dataset
X = np.append(X, randomX, axis=1)
Xstar = np.append(Xstar, randomX, axis=1)
# Measurement error
X += rng.normal(0, 0.5, X.shape)
Xstar += rng.normal(0, 0.5, Xstar.shape)

And we can simply overlay neutral mutations of the resulting tree-sequence (with a realistic mutation rate, now). As before, simulating the mutations for the entire population can be slow. Please, refer to the concept of simplification to see how you can scale it further.

next_id = pyslim.next_slim_mutation_id(ts)
mts = msprime.sim_mutations(
    ts, rate=1e-8,
    model=msprime.SLiMMutationModel(type=0, next_id=next_id),
    keep=True,
)
print(f"The tree sequence now has {mts.num_mutations} mutations,\n"
      f"and mean pairwise nucleotide diversity is {mts.diversity():0.3e}.")
The tree sequence now has 881148 mutations,
and mean pairwise nucleotide diversity is 1.327e-04.

Let’s discard all SNPs with a MAF2 below 0.05:

def maf(variant):
    freqs = np.array([x for x in variant.frequencies().values()])
    freqs.sort()
    return freqs[-2]
discard = np.where([maf(variant) < 0.05 for variant in mts.variants()])
Y = genotype_matrix(mts.delete_sites(discard))
Y.shape
(2808, 22986)
np.savez(
    "../genomic_offsets/datasets/empirical.npz", 
    X = X, Xstar = Xstar,
    Y = Y, wstar=wstar
)
